conduct another underground detonation in its effort to learn how to make nuclear arms \u2014 its sixth explosive test in a decade and perhaps its most powerful yet.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">North Korea\u2019s nuclear tests have grown steadily more destructive, and the country continues to pursue its longtime goal of putting a nuclear warhead on an intercontinental missile capable of <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2017/03/04/world/asia/north-korea-missile-program-sabotage.html\">reaching targets around the globe</a>.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">The United States <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2017/04/09/world/asia/korean-peninsula-us-aircraft-carrier-north-korea.html\">recently ordered</a> an aircraft carrier and other warships toward the Korean Peninsula in a show of force intended to <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2017/04/12/world/asia/trump-china-north-korea-xi-jinping.html\">discourage the North</a> from testing a nuclear weapon.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">While examining satellite imagery, experts have observed a wide range of activity at Mount Mantap, a mile-high peak where North Korea conducts its nuclear tests. Beneath the mountain, a system of tunnels has been excavated for the past five detonations of the North\u2019s nuclear bombs.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">North Korea often <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2017/03/21/world/asia/north-korea-nuclear-provocations-messages.html?_r=0\">marks significant dates</a> with shows of military force, and analysts say it might detonate a nuclear weapon to celebrate the birthday this Saturday of the nation\u2019s founder, Kim Il-sung, the grandfather of the current leader, Kim Jong-un.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-subhed \">\n    \n\n      <h2>Big debris pile suggests the possibility of a much larger detonation.</h2>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">Since late 2013, piles of rocky debris from the excavation of the site\u2019s North tunnel system have grown quite large \u2014 now big enough to cover a football field, and quite high. It\u2019s <a href=\"http://38north.org/2017/03/punggye031017/\">the largest pile ever observed</a> there.   <p class=\"g-body\">So too, observers of the test site have <a href=\"http://38north.org/2017/04/punggye040617/\">recently noted</a> a lot of water being pumped out of the North tunnel system \u2013 class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">Scientists at the Los Alamos weapons lab who have studied images of the large debris pile <a href=\"http://38north.org/2017/03/punggye031017/\">recently concluded</a> that Mount Mantap could withstand a nuclear explosion of up to 282 kilotons \u2013 roughly 20 times stronger than the Hiroshima blast. Previously, the largest detonations were in the Hiroshima range.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">No one outside of North Korea knows for sure what could take place or how big the blast might be. It\u2019s a guessing game \u2014 though a sophisticated one for Washington\u2019s intelligence agencies, and less so for civilians armed only with unclassified information. Either way, it's a detective story full of clues, questions and a protagonist with a clear motive.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">Experts in satellite imagery, military analysts around the world, and geologists and physicists track progress at the remote site mainly through the observation of tunneling, building construction, truck movements and, though harder to see, personnel moves.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-ad \">\n    \n\n      <div class=\"g-ad-wrapper\"></div>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-subhed \">\n    \n\n      <h2>Seismic activity helps pinpoint North Korea\u2019s detonations.</h2>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">Mount Mantap is the world\u2019s only active nuclear test site. Most other nuclear states long ago gave up such explosions in <a href=\"https://www.state.gov/t/avc/c42328.htm\">a coordinated effort</a> to end arms races and their dangerous and costly spirals of military action and reaction.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">In North Korea, the test devices are buried deep inside tunnels bored through solid rock far below Mount Mantap\u2019s peaks, creating field labs for nuclear experiments. The nearest major city, Chongjin, is situated about 50 miles to the northeast.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">The tunnels for the North\u2019s tests are excavated just over halfway up Mount Mantap, which rises to a height of 7,234 feet. The bomb is placed at the end of the tunnel, which gets partly backfilled to prevent radioactive leakage, and then detonated in the test.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item (function() {\n        // only want one resizer on the page\n        if (document.documentElement.className.indexOf(\"g-resizer-v3-init\") > -1) return;\n        document.documentElement.className += \" g-resizer-v3-init\";\n        // require IE9+\n        if (!(\"querySelector\" in document)) return;\n        function resizer() {\n            var elements = Array.prototype.slice.call(document.querySelectorAll(\".g-artboard-v3[data-min-width]\")),\n                widthById = {};\n            elements.forEach(function(el) {\n                var parent = el.parentNode,\n                    width = widthById[parent.id] || parent.getBoundingClientRect().width,\n                    minwidth = el.getAttribute(\"data-min-width\"),\n                    maxwidth = el.getAttribute(\"data-max-width\");\n                widthById[parent.id] = width;\n\n                if (+minwidth <= width && (+maxwidth >= width || maxwidth === null)) {\n                    var img = el.querySelector('.g-aiImg');\n                    if (img.getAttribute('data-src') && img.getAttribute('src') != img.getAttribute('data-src')) {\n                        img.setAttribute('src', img.getAttribute('data-src'));\n                    }\n                    el.style.display = \"block\";\n                } else {\n                    el.style.display = \"none\";\n                }\n            });\n            try {\n                if (window.parent && window.parent.$) {\n                    window.parent.$(\"body\").trigger(\"resizedcontent\", [window]);\n                }\n                document.documentElement.dispatchEvent(new Event('resizedcontent'));\n                if (window.require && document.querySelector('meta[name=sourceApp]') && document.querySelector('meta[name=sourceApp]').content == 'nyt-v5') {\n                    require(['foundation/main'], function() {\n                        require(['shared/interactive/instances/app-communicator'], function(AppCommunicator) {\n                            AppCommunicator.triggerResize();\n                        });\n                    });\n                }\n            } catch(e) { console.log(e); }\n        }\n\n        resizer();\n        document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', resizer);\n        // feel free to replace throttle with _.throttle, if available\n        window.addEventListener('resize', throttle(resizer, 200));        \n\n        function throttle(func, wait) {\n            // from underscore.js\n            var _now = Date.now || function() { return new Date().getTime(); },\n                context, args, result, timeout = null, previous = 0;\n            var later = function() {\n                previous = _now();\n                timeout = null;\n                result = func.apply(context, args);\n                if (!timeout) context = args = null;\n            };\n            return function() {\n                var now = _now(), remaining = wait - (now - previous);\n                context = this;\n                args = arguments;\n                if (remaining <= 0 || remaining > wait) {\n                    if (timeout) {\n                        clearTimeout(timeout);\n                        timeout = null;\n                    }\n                    previous = now;\n                    result = func.apply(context, args);\n                    if (!timeout) context = args = null;\n                } else if (!timeout) {\n                    timeout = setTimeout(later, remaining);\n                }\n                return result;\n            };\n        }\n\n       \n    })();\n</script>\n\n\t<!-- End ai2html - 2017-04-10 - 11:04 -->\n</div>\n tests to increase the amount of time for bomb makers to conduct detailed analyses of the blasts and learn from mistakes.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">\u201cThey\u2019ve done five tests in 10 years,\u201d said Siegfried S. Hecker, a Stanford professor who once directed the Los Alamos weapons laboratory in New Mexico, the birthplace of the atomic bomb. \u201cYou can learn a lot in that time.\u201d</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">In contrast, he added, India and Pakistan conducted <a href=\"https://www.armscontrol.org/act/2008_05/lookingback\">a rush of nuclear detonations</a> in May 1998 in what experts called a blitz of saber rattling. \u201cThey couldn\u2019t have learned much,\u201d Dr. Hecker said.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-ad \">\n    \n\n      <div class=\"g-ad-wrapper\"></div>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-subhed \">\n    \n\n      <h2>North Korea may be focused on increasing the power and range of its nuclear weapons.</h2>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">North Korea\u2019s past tests are thought to have centered on mastering <a href=\"http://tutorials.nti.org/nuclear-101/nuclear-weapons/\">a simple type</a> of atomic bomb, known as an implosion device.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">Some of the tests may have featured \u201cboosted\u201d atomic bombs, however \u2014 meaning that an injection of tritium, a radioactive form of hydrogen, could have increased their destructive power. All such fuels, known as thermonuclear, need the high heats from an exploding atom bomb for ignition.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">\u201cIt\u2019s possible that North Korea has already boosted,\u201d said Gregory S. Jones, a scientist at the Rand Corporation who analyzes nuclear issues. Like other experts, he pointed to the nation\u2019s two nuclear detonations last year as possible tests of small boosted arms.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\">As signs of the North\u2019s interest in boosting, <a href=\"http://isis-online.org/uploads/isis-reports/documents/Update_on_North_Koreas_Yongbyon_Nuclear_Site_September15_2015_Final.pdf\">experts cite</a> modifications to a reactor that could make tritium, as well as <a href=\"http://isis-online.org/uploads/isis-reports/documents/Sept_2016_Yongbyon_Update_20Sept2016_Final.pdf\">construction of a plant</a> that could gather up the radioactive gas. Boosted arms can raise the destructive power of atomic blasts or greatly reduce their need for atomic fuel. That savings can significantly lighten and shrink the resulting arms, making them <a href=\"https://fas.org/blogs/security/2016/01/north-korea-nuclear/\">easier to hurl </a>over long distances. Boosting is considered a main step to scaling down warheads so they can fit atop intercontinental missiles.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-text \">\n    \n\n      <p class=\"g-body\"><a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2016/12/23/world/nuclear-weapon-countries.html\">Nine countries</a> possess nuclear arms, most having advanced over time from making simple atom bombs to advanced hydrogen bombs. <strong>Nuclear experts say North Korea\u2019s program is at an intermediate phase of development \u2014 somewhere between stages one and three.</strong> The secret to achieving more destructive power is to raise the amount of thermonuclear fuel that an exploding atom bomb can ignite.</p>\n\n\n    </div>\n\n    \n\n    \n      <div class=\"g-item g-column \">\n    \n\n      <div class=\"g-column-container\">\n  \n    <div class=\"g-column-col\" style=\"width: calc(25% - 20px);\"> \n     \n\n      <p class=\"g-column-hed 0\"><span class=\"bombhed\"><span class=\"bomb-stage\">STAGE 1</span> <br> Implosion Atom Bomb </span> <br> <span class=\"bombtype\"> uses conventional explosives to compress and ignite atomic fuel </span></p>\n\n      <p class=\"g-column-text 0\"><span class=\"hiroshima-label dpower\"><strong>Destructive power</strong></span><br><br><span class=\"hiroshima\"><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trinity_(nuclear_test)\">1</a></span> <br> <span class=\"hiroshima-label\">Hiroshima</span></p>\n\n       \n        <div class=\"g-column-asset\">\n          <div hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"Inv2\" class=\"ad inv2-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"Inv3\" class=\"ad inv3-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"ab1\" class=\"ad ab1-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"ab2\" class=\"ad ab2-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"ab3\" class=\"ad ab3-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"prop1\" class=\"ad prop1-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"prop2\" class=\"ad prop2-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"Anchor\" class=\"ad anchor-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"ADX_CLIENTSIDE\" class=\"ad adx-clientside-ad hidden\"></div>\n</body>\n</html>\n", "title": "North Korea May Be Preparing Its 6th Nuclear Test", "url": "https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/04/12/world/asia/north-korea-nuclear-test.html", "date": "04-15-2017", "source": "the-new-york-times", "text": "New satellite images suggest that North Korea might soon conduct another underground detonation in its effort to learn how to make nuclear arms \u2014 its sixth explosive test in a decade and perhaps its most powerful yet.\n\nNorth Korea\u2019s nuclear tests have grown steadily more destructive, and the country continues to pursue its longtime goal of putting a nuclear warhead on an intercontinental missile capable of reaching targets around the globe.\n\nThe United States recently ordered an aircraft carrier and other warships toward the Korean Peninsula in a show of force intended to discourage the North from testing a nuclear weapon.\n\nWhile examining satellite imagery, experts have observed a wide range of activity at Mount Mantap, a mile-high peak where North Korea conducts its nuclear tests. Beneath the mountain, a system of tunnels has been excavated for the past five detonations of the North\u2019s nuclear bombs.\n\nNorth Korea often marks significant dates with shows of military force, and analysts say it might detonate a nuclear weapon to celebrate the birthday this Saturday of the nation\u2019s founder, Kim Il-sung, the grandfather of the current leader, Kim Jong-un.\n\nBig debris pile suggests the possibility of a much larger detonation.\n\nSince late 2013, piles of rocky debris from the excavation of the site\u2019s North tunnel system have grown quite large \u2014 now big enough to cover a football field, and quite high. It\u2019s the largest pile ever observed there. Work on the excavation has recently slowed, quite likely signaling readiness for the next detonation.\n\nJan. 5, 2017 Jan. 22, 2017 Additional debris Entrance to north tunnel 125 feet Jan. 5, 2017 Jan. 22, 2017 Additional debris Entrance to north tunnel 125 feet Jan. 5, 2017 Jan. 22, 2017 Additional debris Entrance to north tunnel 125 feet Jan. 5, 2017 Entrance to north tunnel 125 feet Jan. 22, 2017 Additional debris Source: DigitalGlobe Imagery\n\nSo too, observers of the test site have recently noted a lot of water being pumped out of the North tunnel system \u2013 presumably to prevent washouts and keep it dry for test instrumentation. Groundwater is often a problem in tunneling as it can slow progress, weaken structures and cause shorts in electrical gear.\n\nMarch 28, 2017 Jan. 22, 2017 Entrance to north tunnel Water drained from tunnel Water continued to be pumped out 325 feet March 28, 2017 Jan. 22, 2017 Entrance to north tunnel Water drained from tunnel Water continued to be pumped out 325 feet Jan. 22, 2017 March 28, 2017 North tunnel entrance Water continued to be pumped out Water drained from tunnel 325 feet Jan. 22, 2017 March 28, 2017 Entrance to north tunnel Water continued to be pumped out Water drained from tunnel 325 feet Source: DigitalGlobe Imagery\n\nScientists at the Los Alamos weapons lab who have studied images of the large debris pile recently concluded that Mount Mantap could withstand a nuclear explosion of up to 282 kilotons \u2013 roughly 20 times stronger than the Hiroshima blast. Previously, the largest detonations were in the Hiroshima range.\n\nNo one outside of North Korea knows for sure what could take place or how big the blast might be. It\u2019s a guessing game \u2014 though a sophisticated one for Washington\u2019s intelligence agencies, and less so for civilians armed only with unclassified information. Either way, it's a detective story full of clues, questions and a protagonist with a clear motive.\n\nExperts in satellite imagery, military analysts around the world, and geologists and physicists track progress at the remote site mainly through the observation of tunneling, building construction, truck movements and, though harder to see, personnel moves.\n\nSeismic activity helps pinpoint North Korea\u2019s detonations.\n\nMount Mantap is the world\u2019s only active nuclear test site. Most other nuclear states long ago gave up such explosions in a coordinated effort to end arms races and their dangerous and costly spirals of military action and reaction.\n\nIn North Korea, the test devices are buried deep inside tunnels bored through solid rock far below Mount Mantap\u2019s peaks, creating field labs for nuclear experiments. The nearest major city, Chongjin, is situated about 50 miles to the northeast.\n\nThe tunnels for the North\u2019s tests are excavated just over halfway up Mount Mantap, which rises to a height of 7,234 feet. The bomb is placed at the end of the tunnel, which gets partly backfilled to prevent radioactive leakage, and then detonated in the test.\n\nMT. This design is believed to have come from Pakistani nuclear test sites. 0.5 miles Satellite Image by CNES / Astrium via Google Earth | Magnitude data from the Nuclear Threat Initiative and USGS Note: Tunnel system based upon an analysis by Frank Pabian and Siegfried Hecker.\n\nExperts say the North\u2019s tunnel system will quite likely play a significant role in future testing because it is the busiest area at the Punggye-ri nuclear test site, has a conspicuous perimeter fence and has the largest amount of protective rock directly overhead.\n\nSeveral independent teams, including ones from China, South Korea, Norway and the United States, have gathered seismic readings from the North\u2019s bomb tests. In addition, a world body known as the the Comprehensive Test Ban Treaty Organization, based in Vienna, Austria, operates a sprawling network of global sensors.\n\nLast year, a team of South Korean scientists confirmed a test\u2019s exact location within Mount Mantap by studying data from a radar satellite that detected subtle elevation changes on the mountain\u2019s surface.\n\nSo far, North Korea\u2019s nuclear tests have grown more destructive.\n\nCircles sized by destructive power 35 17 17 8 2 kilotons 2007 2008 2009 2010 2011 2012 2013 2014 2015 2016 Circles sized by destructive power 35 17 17 8 2 kilotons 2007 2008 2009 2010 2011 2012 2013 2014 2015 2016 Circles sized by destructive power 35 17 17 8 2 kilotons Oct. 2006 May 2009 Feb. 2013 Jan. 2016 Circles sized by destructive power 35 17 17 8 2 kilotons Oct. 2006 May 2009 Feb. 2013 Jan. 2016 Source: Nuclear Threat Initiative\n\nNote: Destructive power numbers represent worst case estimates.\n\nThe North has shown technical savvy in pacing its nuclear tests to increase the amount of time for bomb makers to conduct detailed analyses of the blasts and learn from mistakes.\n\n\u201cThey\u2019ve done five tests in 10 years,\u201d said Siegfried S. Hecker, a Stanford professor who once directed the Los Alamos weapons laboratory in New Mexico, the birthplace of the atomic bomb. \u201cYou can learn a lot in that time.\u201d\n\nIn contrast, he added, India and Pakistan conducted a rush of nuclear detonations in May 1998 in what experts called a blitz of saber rattling. \u201cThey couldn\u2019t have learned much,\u201d Dr. Hecker said.\n\nNorth Korea may be focused on increasing the power and range of its nuclear weapons.\n\nNorth Korea\u2019s past tests are thought to have centered on mastering a simple type of atomic bomb, known as an implosion device.\n\nSome of the tests may have featured \u201cboosted\u201d atomic bombs, however \u2014 meaning that an injection of tritium, a radioactive form of hydrogen, could have increased their destructive power. All such fuels, known as thermonuclear, need the high heats from an exploding atom bomb for ignition.\n\n\u201cIt\u2019s possible that North Korea has already boosted,\u201d said Gregory S. Jones, a scientist at the Rand Corporation who analyzes nuclear issues. Like other experts, he pointed to the nation\u2019s two nuclear detonations last year as possible tests of small boosted arms.\n\nAs signs of the North\u2019s interest in boosting, experts cite modifications to a reactor that could make tritium, as well as construction of a plant that could gather up the radioactive gas. Boosted arms can raise the destructive power of atomic blasts or greatly reduce their need for atomic fuel. That savings can significantly lighten and shrink the resulting arms, making them easier to hurl over long distances. Boosting is considered a main step to scaling down warheads so they can fit atop intercontinental missiles.\n\nNine countries possess nuclear arms, most having advanced over time from making simple atom bombs to advanced hydrogen bombs. Nuclear experts say North Korea\u2019s program is at an intermediate phase of development \u2014 somewhere between stages one and three. The secret to achieving more destructive power is to raise the amount of thermonuclear fuel that an exploding atom bomb can ignite.\n\nSTAGE 1\n\nImplosion Atom Bomb\n\nuses conventional explosives to compress and ignite atomic fuel Destructive power\n\n\n\n1\n\nHiroshima Explosive layer Atomic fuel Explosive layer Atomic fuel Explosive layer Atomic fuel Explosive layer Atomic fuel Explosive layer Atomic fuel STAGE 2\n\nBoosted Atom Bomb\n\nuses a bit of thermonuclear fuel inside the atomic core\n\n\n\n3\n\nHiroshimas Thermonuclear gas Thermonuclear gas Thermonuclear gas Thermonuclear gas Thermonuclear gas STAGE 3\n\nLayered Atom Bomb\n\nuses more thermonuclear fuel outside the atomic core\n\n\n\n25\n\nHiroshimas Solid thermonuclear fuel Solid thermonuclear fuel Solid thermonuclear fuel Solid thermonuclear fuel Solid thermonuclear fuel STAGE 4\n\nHydrogen Bomb\n\nuses lots of hydrogen fuel that the nearby atom core ignites\n\n\n\n1,000\n\nHiroshimas Atom bomb Solid thermonuclear fuel Atom bomb Solid thermonuclear fuel Atom bomb Solid thermonuclear fuel Atom bomb Solid thermonuclear fuel Atom bomb Solid thermonuclear fuel\n\nNote: Destructive power for each stage is based on early tests in the US and USSR, not on current stockpiles. Note: Destructive power for each stage is based on early tests in the US and USSR, not on current stockpiles.\n\nExperts say the likelihood of North Korea making strides in its nuclear program has risen with the recent evidence that the nation tried to sell excess lithium-6, which is the main ingredient for making thermonuclear fuels, including tritium.\n\nSo too, satellite images of the mountainous test site reveal the digging of the deep tunnel system, which could allow the detonation of a larger device.\n\nA gathering at the remote site is considered a strong sign.\n\nOn March 28, a satellite image showed people gathered in front of an administrative building at the test site. The last time such a large group was observed was on Jan. 4, 2013, a little more than a month before North Korea\u2019s third nuclear detonation.\n\nMarch 28, 2017 Jan. 4, 2013 People in parade getting closer to detonating a device in its increasingly long series of nuclear tests.", "images":
